Now I understand the structure. The BufferSyncSupport:\n- Has a `listen()` method that sets up listeners for document changes\n- Has a `getErr()` method to request diagnostics for specific files\n- Has a `handles()` method to check if a resource is a synced buffer\n- Only tracks open documents in `syncedBuffers`\n\nThe issue is that when files change on disk (via git checkout), if they're not open in the editor, they won't be in `syncedBuffers`, so:\n1. The signature is:\n```\nexport function createFileSystemWatcher(globPattern: GlobPattern, ignoreCreateEvents?: boolean, ignoreChangeEvents?: boolean, ignoreDeleteEvents?: boolean): FileSystemWatcher;\n```\n\nSo my usage is:\n```\ncreateFileSystemWatcher('**/*.{ts,tsx,js,jsx,mts,mjs,cts,cjs}', false, false, true)\n```\n\nThis means:\n- globPattern: '**/*.{ts,tsx,js,jsx,mts,mjs,cts,cjs}' ✓\n- ignoreCreateEvents: false (don't ignore create events)\n- ignoreChangeEvents: false (don't ignore change events) ✓ - we want to listen to changes\n- ignoreDeleteEvents: true (ignore delete events) ✓ - we don't need to handle deletes in this watcher since bufferSyncSupport.onDelete already handles them\n\nThis looks correct! However, I realize there might be one issue: if a file is open in the editor but the user hasn't made any changes to it (the file changed on disk but not through the editor), we should still refresh diagnostics. Let me reconsider the logic.\n\nActually, looking at my code again:\n```typescript\nfileWatcher.onDidChange(resource => {\n    // Only refresh diagnostics for files that are not currently open in the editor\n    if (!this.bufferSyncSupport.handles(resource)) {\n        this.bufferSyncSupport.getErr([resource]);\n    }\n});\n```\n\nThis only refreshes diagnostics if the file is NOT open. But if the file IS open, the diagnostics should be automatically refreshed when the file is saved (since saving triggers onDidChangeTextDocument). However, if the file is open but changes on disk (e.g., due to git checkout), the in-memory version won't match the disk version, which is a different problem.\n\n\nFor a git branch switch scenario, files are typically closed before the switch occurs, so my approach should handle this correctly. I should also consider performance implications—watching all TypeScript/JavaScript files could be resource-intensive in large workspaces, though VS Code is designed to handle this. Additionally, there shouldn't be duplicate diagnostics refreshes since the watcher and text document changes operate independently.\n\nDeleted files are already managed by bufferSyncSupport.onDelete, so I can safely ignore those events. One thing I need to verify is whether bufferSyncSupport is initialized before the file watcher starts firing events. Looking at the setup, bufferSyncSupport is instantiated first, then listen() is called in onReady, and the delete handler is registered immediately after. This ordering should be fine since the watcher won't emit events until the extension is fully loaded.\n\nThe fileWatcher callback references bufferSyncSupport.handles() and bufferSyncSupport.getErr(), which should both be available at runtime. I should confirm that getErr() can be invoked right away without waiting for the listen() call to complete.\n\nLooking at getErr(), it filters resources through the handles() check before processing them, so calling it with unhandled resources is safe—they'll simply be filtered out and the method returns early if nothing matches. The implementation appears sound, so I'll verify the syntax This should be sufficient.\n\n2. Performance: Creating a file watcher for all these files might have some performance impact, but it's a standard operation in VS Code.\n\n3. If a file is open, the file:// content might still be the old in-memory version, and the disk content is the new version from git. But VS Code should detect this and prompt the user to reload the file.
